        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141184890" href="/book/show/3774.The_Subterraneans">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who enjoy Kerouac or are into that sort of ideal.</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35330732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer774710780789918394">I really loved this book. Jack Kerouac, for all his iconic writing and cliche beatnick status, still manages to capture the life and thoughts of the younger generation who were simply looking for any sort of adventure. That is, they have their own dreams and attempt to reach them. Kerouac shows their growth and takes his readers through the realization that things aren't like they expected them to be. Somehow, though, even though this sort of melancholic epiphany seems to be common for his endin</span>
  <span id="freeText774710780789918394" style="display:none">I really loved this book. Jack Kerouac, for all his iconic writing and cliche beatnick status, still manages to capture the life and thoughts of the younger generation who were simply looking for any sort of adventure. That is, they have their own dreams and attempt to reach them. Kerouac shows their growth and takes his readers through the realization that things aren't like they expected them to be. Somehow, though, even though this sort of melancholic epiphany seems to be common for his endings (at least in the books of his I have read), he manages to leave me with a sense of understanding, to leave me with the feeling that everything is alright. He approaches lost-love like an experience. <br />Overall, I really liked this book. The lack of true sentence structure made it difficult to read in small bursts, so I suggest devoting large portions of time to it if you are going to read it. It took me almost three weeks of reading in-between classes to finish it. <br />I highly suggest this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="774710780789918394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_538895424" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/538895424" />
    <a title="mwpm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13827810-mwpm"><img alt="mwpm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516943741p2/13827810.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/538895424?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mwpm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mwpm" href="/user/show/13827810-mwpm">mwpm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802195715" href="/book/show/10546748-the-subterraneans">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13827810-mwpm?shelf=novels-novellas">novels-novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer538895424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7802626463952966590">The protagonists of Jack Kerouac's <i>The Subterraneans</i> and Richard Brautigan's <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/379499.Sombrero_Fallout' title='Sombrero Fallout by Richard Brautigan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sombrero Fallout</a>
</i> (both versions of their respective authors) have lost or will lose a love interest. This loss will serve as the catalyst for the text's composition. Kerouac's <i>The Subterraneans</i> ends, like Gertrude Stein's <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/527495.The_Autobiography_of_Alice_B__Toklas' title='The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas by Gertrude Stein' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas</a>
</i>, with the assertion that they, the author, will record or have recorded these events, thereby creating a text that seems to write itself. Brautigan's <i>Sombre</i></span>
  <span id="freeText7802626463952966590" style="display:none">The protagonists of Jack Kerouac's <i>The Subterraneans</i> and Richard Brautigan's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/379499.Sombrero_Fallout" title="Sombrero Fallout by Richard Brautigan" rel="nofollow noopener">Sombrero Fallout</a>
</i> (both versions of their respective authors) have lost or will lose a love interest. This loss will serve as the catalyst for the text's composition. Kerouac's <i>The Subterraneans</i> ends, like Gertrude Stein's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/527495.The_Autobiography_of_Alice_B__Toklas" title="The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas by Gertrude Stein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Autobiography of Alice B. Toklas</a>
</i>, with the assertion that they, the author, will record or have recorded these events, thereby creating a text that seems to write itself. Brautigan's <i>Sombrero Fallout</i> begins with the shredding of the first page of a story (about a sombrero falling from the sky). Despite being torn up, the story takes on a life of its own, literally writing itself from the bottom of a wastepaper basket. <br /><br />Whereas Kerouac's <i>The Subterraneans</i> is an exercise in style, Brautigan's <i>Sombrero Fallout</i> is more substantial than one would gather from its simplistic style. The discarded story embodies the neglect that the protagonist (an unnamed American humorist) must feel following his separation from Yukiko (his Japanese ex-girlfriend). The story, like the relationship, could have developed into something beautiful. Instead, in its neglect, it has festered into something ugly: the insomnia of the American humorist and the violent turmoil of the story in the wastepaper basket. <br /><br />A more significant form of neglect is perpetrated by both authors. Despite lost love being at the heart of both novels, there is a deplorable neglect for the women who serve as the love interests. It is worth considering that both women have been drawn from the author's lives, and that both women belong to cultures fetishized by the authors. <br /><br />Kerouac based Mardou Fox on African and Native American intellectual Alene Lee. Brautigan based Yukiko on Japanese musician Anne Kuniyuki. Kerouac fetishized African American culture, and wrote in a style inspired by Jazz. Brautigan fetishized Japanese style, and wrote in the style of the Japanese <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/I_Novel" rel="nofollow noopener">I-Novel</a> (with the exception of <i>Sombrero Fallout</i>, which lacks a first person narrator).<br /><br />Kerouac's "Negro" girlfriend and Brautigan's ex-girlfriend (alternatively "Japanese" and "Asian", fortunately never "Oriental") are little more than the embodiment of their cultural fetishization. By which I mean to ask, is it possible for these men to truly know or love these women when they are so preoccupied with their superficial attributes, never pausing to consider the experience of a Japanese woman in America, or an African and Native American woman in America. <br /><br />In <i>Sombrero Fallout</i>, Brautigan wrote<br /><br /><blockquote>Her eyes were dark and narrow he wondered if she could see as much as a Caucasian could. The thought vanished upon the thinking of it but it was to return many times during the two years that he would know her. It was just that he wondered sometimes if she could see everything that was in a room or wherever they were. Maybe she was only seeing 75 percent of what was going on.<br />It was the kind of thought that a child would have. (pg. 40)</blockquote><br /><br />Is this awareness? It is only awareness to the extent that the acknowledgement of ignorance can be awareness ("I know that I know nothing" is a well-known saying that is derived from Plato's account of the Greek philosopher Socrates). Observations like these fail to elevate the characters Mardou Fox and Yukiko above the superficial observations by the author (or author in the guise of the protagonist). They are peripheral characters, decorations adorning the interesting lives of their interesting while male authors.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7802626463952966590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2066467810" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2066467810" />
    <a title="Ananya Ghosh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38180128-ananya-ghosh"><img alt="Ananya Ghosh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456210336p2/38180128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2066467810?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ananya Ghosh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ananya Ghosh" href="/user/show/38180128-ananya-ghosh">Ananya Ghosh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802195715" href="/book/show/10546748-the-subterraneans">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38180128-ananya-ghosh?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38180128-ananya-ghosh?shelf=beat-generation">beat-generation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38180128-ananya-ghosh?shelf=stream-of-consciousness">stream-of-consciousness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38180128-ananya-ghosh?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2066467810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14003454643715683389">I want to give it 2.5 stars, and am waiting for Goodreads to let me do that someday. I'll also admit that this was my first ever Kerouac, first ever Beat generation text, and first ever stream-of-consciousness text, and I'm sorry but I didn't get most of it, especially, the charm behind it all.<br/><br/>When I went into it, I knew a few things. Kerouac is very popular, but is going to be difficult for me; this book isn't one of his most hyped ones; it's a break-up story. And with these few facts, I dived </span>
  <span id="freeText14003454643715683389" style="display:none">I want to give it 2.5 stars, and am waiting for Goodreads to let me do that someday. I'll also admit that this was my first ever Kerouac, first ever Beat generation text, and first ever stream-of-consciousness text, and I'm sorry but I didn't get most of it, especially, the charm behind it all.<br /><br />When I went into it, I knew a few things. Kerouac is very popular, but is going to be difficult for me; this book isn't one of his most hyped ones; it's a break-up story. And with these few facts, I dived in. <br /><br />To me, the protagonist Leo Percepied wasn't one of my most favourite characters. Honestly, I think I only liked his girlfriend (ex) Mardou Fox and his best friend Adam. The story takes the reader on a roller coaster ride from the first time he met her to the time they broke up and most of it is filled with the night life of San Francisco as the characters go bar hopping and partying at just about anyone's place and have intellectual conversations that I didn't really get much. Throughout, Leo rambles on about how he wants Mardou but is constantly evading or mistreating her, and I didn't understand the concept of why. He gets jealous whenever any of his friends or acquaintances show even the slightest interest in her, but he never actively works to keep her, while Mardou seems to be much more faithful. I hated the fact that despite knowing about her poor mental health, he treats her poorly and also makes her skip her appointments to the therapist, repeatedly. He makes promises to meet her, go out with her, but whenever they do go out, he fools around with his other friends, while she sits in the corner doing nothing, and if someone else approaches her, he just sulks in the background, claiming that she's going to leave him one of these days. Of course, she would! I honestly felt so glad that Mardou did finally break up with him because that was a very toxic relationship. <br /><br />While I expected that I would have to throw my grammar down the window for this one, with the stream-of-consciousness, I didn't really like the digressions he made every few lines, it got extremely confusing. But what got even worse and put me off was the plethora of characters, with long names, and such traits and antics, it got so difficult to remember them and get on with the story that by the middle of it, I just went on reading, not caring who was who.<br /><br />So, in the end, I did not care much for the book, but then this was my first read and I'm still hoping to explore and read more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14003454643715683389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_194759743" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194759743" />
    <a title="Simon Kirk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5965958-simon-kirk"><img alt="Simon Kirk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442987877p2/5965958.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194759743?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Kirk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Kirk" href="/user/show/5965958-simon-kirk">Simon Kirk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141184890" href="/book/show/3774.The_Subterraneans">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194759743" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2138239640077848983">As one of the founders of the Beat Generation, author Jack Kerouac is now ironically reaping the rewards for his works back in the 1950s and early '60s, despite his unexpected death in 1969 at the ripe age of 47.<br/><br/><br/><br/>His 'innovative' use of spontaneous prose has been passed on as inspiration for many who dabble in the art of literature, while many modern musicians also stake claim in being influenced from the Lowell, Massachusetts born writer.<br/><br/><br/><br/>The Subterraneans - like many of Kerouac's novels - took l</span>
  <span id="freeText2138239640077848983" style="display:none">As one of the founders of the Beat Generation, author Jack Kerouac is now ironically reaping the rewards for his works back in the 1950s and early '60s, despite his unexpected death in 1969 at the ripe age of 47.<br /><br /><br /><br />His 'innovative' use of spontaneous prose has been passed on as inspiration for many who dabble in the art of literature, while many modern musicians also stake claim in being influenced from the Lowell, Massachusetts born writer.<br /><br /><br /><br />The Subterraneans - like many of Kerouac's novels - took little time to compile. Spanning over 93 pages, this form of writing - as a reader - is quite a hard concept to pierce, with pragmatic sentences and an abundance of block sequences making the novel extremely hard to engage with from the outset.<br /><br /><br /><br />Leo Percipied (Jack Kerouac) tells a story - that in a nutshell - consists of jumping from bars around San Fransisco with his cliquey group, comprising of other aspiring writers of the time, Adam Moorad and Frank Carmody (Allan Ginsberg and William S. Burroughs), while meeting and falling in love with an African American women, Mardou Fox (Alene Lee) who by the end of a the novel “makes it” with one of the outsiders of The Subterraneans, Yuri Gligoric (Gregory Corso).<br /><br /><br /><br />Given the time it was written and the social issues it entails within the relationships of certain characters, The Subterraneans can be seen upon as controversial, despite its intentions not to be. It still doesn't escape the fact that this is self-indulgence at its finest. Maybe that's the whole point of the exercise Kerouac was attempting to design, but there's a fine line in self-indulging and this book crosses it on a regular basis. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://lullabizine.blogspot.com/2008/05/jack-kerouac-subterraneans-groove-press.html">http://lullabizine.blogspot.com/2008/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2138239640077848983" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109264111" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109264111?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TC Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TC Jones" href="/user/show/3308250-tc-jones">TC Jones</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109264111" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4717431501676058023">I really wanted to like this book. I tried my hardest to understand its unique style, its flowing movements lacking punctuation, and the free-form stream of conciousness. The problem was, I just couldn't. The narrative itself wasn't all that exciting; a lot of getting drunk and scoring with women, but not anything substantial that makes a worthwhile story. <br/><br/>I had trouble identifying with the characters or even liking them. They were drunk all the time, driven for nothing in life except sex and se</span>
  <span id="freeText4717431501676058023" style="display:none">I really wanted to like this book. I tried my hardest to understand its unique style, its flowing movements lacking punctuation, and the free-form stream of conciousness. The problem was, I just couldn't. The narrative itself wasn't all that exciting; a lot of getting drunk and scoring with women, but not anything substantial that makes a worthwhile story. <br /><br />I had trouble identifying with the characters or even liking them. They were drunk all the time, driven for nothing in life except sex and sex alone. Nothing but a hedenistic band of eclectic "do-nothings."<br /><br />Even the writing itself was not all that good. The language was dull and lacked description and for long periods at a time meandered off into drug-induced rants that made no real sense to the reader. I found it interesting in a way because I felt like that though these parts were hazy and often unintelligable, it did capture the feeling of being stoned or drunk or both. <br /><br />After reading "On the Road", "The Dharma Bums", and "The Town and The City" I very much had high hopes for this book too. The only problem is that it didn't deliever the raw excitement and adventure of "On the Road" or the self reflective loneliness of "Dharma Bums", or the emotion of "Town and the City". Some people might enjoy it, and I do recommend it for its uniqueness of style, but as a story as a whole it lacks connectivity and direction. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4717431501676058023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_481965036" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/481965036" />
    <a title="Zachary Johnson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12059807-zachary-johnson"><img alt="Zachary Johnson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361941257p2/12059807.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/481965036?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zachary Johnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zachary Johnson" href="/user/show/12059807-zachary-johnson">Zachary Johnson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer481965036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17343621307562615037">Ahh, The beat generation. What an Isle of Misfit Toys. I think from most of the books I have read, the ones from the beat generation are the most interesting. I haven't secured a copy of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/70401.On_the_Road' title='On the Road by Jack Kerouac' rel='nofollow noopener'>On the Road</a> But when I do I will fully judge Jack Kerouac. Moving on to this book. I was rather interested in the main character at the beginning of the book. He is a Vagabond/Sailor/Hippie in the San Francisco area. He has a group of friends that pretty much do nothing more then sit around and drink and smoke T</span>
  <span id="freeText17343621307562615037" style="display:none">Ahh, The beat generation. What an Isle of Misfit Toys. I think from most of the books I have read, the ones from the beat generation are the most interesting. I haven't secured a copy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/70401.On_the_Road" title="On the Road by Jack Kerouac" rel="nofollow noopener">On the Road</a> But when I do I will fully judge Jack Kerouac. Moving on to this book. I was rather interested in the main character at the beginning of the book. He is a Vagabond/Sailor/Hippie in the San Francisco area. He has a group of friends that pretty much do nothing more then sit around and drink and smoke Tea aka Pot. It is a beautiful set up haha. Then he meets this beautiful girl Mardou and he falls in love, but at first she wants nothing to do with him. Mardou is black, but the funny thing is, is that this topic doesn't stir the racial pot at all really. This is pretty much the whole story, ecept throw in some random events and some sex. <br />I really struggled to read this. Not because it was difficult, but because of how bland it was. I had to read it though because I hadn't finished <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/227463.A_Clockwork_Orange" title="A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Orange</a> and needed another book to report on. If it wasn't for that, I wouldn't I have finished it. <br />This book left me with nothing, So I wouldn't recommend it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17343621307562615037" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('99ao0W/bkhpH7pTtPGXxBk8TMWqfWYGCR0WP7kmbDenJvA5InmwX5IZ11MllzX+CHpGQ1syjl9Xjd6etc8N33w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Burroughs from St. Louis, stabbed a man named David Kammerer with a Boy Scout knife and threw his body in the Hudson River. For eight years, Kammerer had fawned over the younger Carr, but that n<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4324036372045694986\" style=\"display:none\">On August 14, 1944, Lucien Carr, a friend of William S. Burroughs from St. Louis, stabbed a man named David Kammerer with a Boy Scout knife and threw his body in the Hudson River. For eight years, Kammerer had fawned over the younger Carr, but that night something happened: either Carr had had enough or he was forced to defend himself.\n\nThe next day, his clothes stained with blood, Carr went to his friends Bill Burroughs and Jack Kerouac for help. Doing so, he involved them in the crime. A few months later, they were caught up in the crime in a different way. \n\nSomething about the murder captivated the Beats, especially Kerouac and Burroughs, who decided to collaborate on a novel about the events of the previous summer. At the time, the two authors were still unknown, yet to write anything of note. Narrating alternating chapters, they pieced together a hard-boiled tale of bohemian New York during World War II, full of drugs and art, obsession and violence, with scenes and characters drawn from their own lives.\n\nThey submitted their manuscript—called And the Hippos Were Boiled in Their Tanks after an absurd line from a radio bulletin about a circus fire—to publishers, but it was rejected and confined to a filing cabinet for decades. Finally published, at long last, And the Hippos Were Boiled in Their Tanks tells the story of Ramsay Allen and the object of his fixation, the charismatic, idealistic young Phillip Tourian. Phillip and his friends drink and dream in the bars and apartments of the West Village, until, with his friend Mike Ryko (Kerouac\'s narrator), he hatches a plan to ship out as a merchant marine. They\'ll catch a boat for France and jump ship, then make their way through the front to Paris. \n\nAnd the Hippos Were Boiled in Their Tanks is an engaging, fast-paced read that shows the two authors\' developing styles. It is also an incomparable artifact, a legendary novel from the dawn of the Beat movement by two hugely influential writers.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4324036372045694986\" href=\"#\" Burroughs wrote Junky, his first novel. It is a candid eye-witness account of times and places that are now long gone, an unvarnished field report from the American post-war underground. Unafraid to portray himself in 1953 as a confirmed member of two socially-despised under classes (a narcotics addict and a homosexual), Burroughs was writing as a trained anthropologist when he unapologetically described a way of life - in New York, New Orleans, and Mexico City - that by the 1940\'s was already demonized by the artificial anti-drug hysteria of an opportunistic bureaucracy and a cynical, prostrate media. For this fiftieth-anniversary edition, eminent Burroughs scholar Oliver Harris has painstakingly recreated the author\'s original text, word by word, from archival typescripts and places the book\'s contents against a lively historical background in a comprehensive introduction. Here as well, for the first time, are Burroughs\' own unpublished introduction and an entire omitted chapter, along with many &quot;lost&quot; passages, as well as auxiliary texts by Allen Ginsberg Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una g<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8980813240945566411\" style=\"display:none\">Escrita en 1952 pero no publicada hasta 1985 debido a su franca plasmación del deseo homosexual, &quot;Queer&quot;, obra temprana de William S. Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una grotesca fantasía tragicómica y una ingeniosa novela política. Un libro que proporciona muchas claves fundamentales para adentrarse en el arrollador universo literario del autor. Esta edición definitiva, editada con motivo del 25 aniversario de su primera publicación, incorpora una extensa y documentada introducción de Oliver Harris en la que se repasan las complejas circunstancias personales en las que la obra fue escrita y que marcarían la vida y la posterior trayectoria literaria de Burroughs. Y se incluye también, a modo de epílogo, el texto que el propio autor escribió como prólogo para la edición de 1985.\n\n&quot;Queer&quot; está ambientada en un inmenso suburbio, que Burroughs definiría más tarde como la «Interzona», y que abarca desde la Ciudad de México, capital mundial del delito, hasta Panamá. Un álter ego del escritor, Lee, teje su tela amorosa en torno a Allerton, un joven ambiguo, indiferente como un animal. Deambula por locales cada vez más sórdidos, en los que pulula una fauna en estado de descomposición, y en esas excursiones, como un pícaro alienado, nos regala astillas radiactivas de su negrísimo humor. Para resolver sus obsesiones mortíferas y sexuales, Lee parte con su amigo a la búsqueda de la ayahuasca, droga absoluta capaz de otorgar el control total sobre los cerebros, y por eso mismo codiciada por Rusia y Estados Unidos... y por todo adicto. Dispuesto a abismarse en todos los peligros, como un santo o un criminal con orden de búsqueda y captura, Lee no tiene nada que perder. En esta novela aflora por primera vez ese paisaje alucinado que hoy todo lector reconoce como el mundo particular de William S. Burroughs.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8980813240945566411\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer While Kerouac traveled to Tangiers, San Francisco, and Mexico City, Johnson roamed the streets of the East Village, where she found herself in the midst of the cultural revolution the Beats had created. It was praised by The Orlando Sentinel &quot;for the insights it offers to Dylan at work ... from young upstart to grand old man of rock \'n\' roll.&quot; Sounes\'s prodigious research has resulted in new insights on every aspect of Dylan\'s life. His is the only biography to seriously address the past twenty years of Dylan\'s life, leading up to the extraordinary recent releases Time Out of Mind and Love and Theft. He has obtained exclusive information to provide the clearest picture yet of Dylan\'s 1966 motorcycle accident and subsequent &quot;lost years&quot; in Woodstock, New York, and he uncovered the star\'s unknown second marriage. He gives inside accounts of the tours, the creation of every album and the most celebrated songs, Dylan\'s labyrinthine love life, his life-threatening heart illness in 1997, and more -- directly from interviews with girlfriends, family, friends, producers, concert promoters, and fellow musicians. Candid, refreshing, and written with a sincere appreciation of Dylan\'s music and influence, Down the Highway is an essential book for the millions of people who have enjoyed Dylan\'s music over the years. &quot;Dylan comes alive.... Sounes has added a wealth of new information to Dylan studies.&quot; -- Perry Meisel, The New York Times Book Review &quot;Fascinating and finely written.&quot; -- Ronald Radosh, The New Republic &quot;Convincing ... He was also the dynamic leader of war protesters, artists, Flower Power hippies, musicians, punks, and political radicals.\n\nThe Essential Ginsberg collects a mosaic of materials that displays the full range of Ginsberg’s mental landscape. His most important poems, songs, essays, letters, journals, and interviews are displayed in chronological order. His poetic masterpieces, “Howl” and “Kaddish,” are presented here along with lesser-known and difficult to find songs and prose. Personal correspondence with William Burroughs and Jack Kerouac is included as well as photographs—shot and captioned by Ginsberg himself—of his friends and fellow rogues William Burroughs, Neal Cassady, and more.\n\nThrough his essays, journals, interviews, and letters, this definitive volume will inspire readers to delve deeper into a body of work that remains one of the most impressive           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1742.Jack_Kerouac"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1544568646p3/1742.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1742.Jack_Kerouac">Jack Kerouac
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
9,341
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13886047698637342135">Jack Kerouac was born Jean-Louis Lebris de Kerouac on March 12, 1922, in Lowell, Massachusetts. Jack Kerouac's writing career began in the 1940s, but didn't meet with commercial success until 1957, when <i>On the Road</i> was published. The book became an American classic that defined the Beat Generation. Kerouac died on October 21, 1969, from an abdominal hemorrhage, at age 47.<br/><br/><br/></span>

</div>

These writings reveal what Kerouac was thinking, doing, and dreaming during his formative years, and reflect his primary literary influences. Wrapped in a spiritual atmosphere that expresses the yearnings of Kerouac to find himself, &quot;Tristessa&quot;, translated by Jorge García- Robles, a specialist in the beat generation, is the story of the strange loving relationship that the author had with Esperanza, as well as the significant description of the atmosphere that surrounded it, which depicts some key places of Mexico City back then.Hero of the beat generation, the creator of a model of life that would be followed by thousands of young people in the entire world, a sui generis mystic, &quot;Tristessa&quot;, which until recently was not known in Spanish and that was published in English, is one of his fresher and better achieved works. \n\nTristessa es el nombre con el que Kerouac bautizó a Esperanza Villanueva, una joven mexicana católica, prostituta y adicta a ciertas drogas, de quien se enamoró durante una de sus estancias en México, país que visitaba con frecuencia, a mediados de los años cincuenta.
